<p><b>Herbert Clark Hoover</b> (August 10, 1874 – October 20, 1964) was the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="List of Presidents of the United States">31st</a> <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President of the United States</a> (1929–1933). He was a professional <a href="/wiki/Mining_engineer" title="Mining engineer" class="mw-redirect">mining engineer</a> and was raised as a <a href="/wiki/Quaker" title="Quaker" class="mw-redirect">Quaker</a>. A <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican</a>, Hoover served as head of the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Food_Administration" title="U.S. Food Administration" class="mw-redirect">U.S. Food Administration</a> during <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, and became internationally known for humanitarian relief efforts in war-time <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup> As the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_Commerce" title="United States Secretary of Commerce">United States Secretary of Commerce</a> in the 1920s under Presidents <a href="/wiki/Warren_G._Harding" title="Warren G. Harding">Warren G. Harding</a> and <a href="/wiki/Calvin_Coolidge" title="Calvin Coolidge">Calvin Coolidge</a>, he promoted partnerships between government and business under the rubric "economic modernization". In <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1928" title="United States presidential election, 1928">the presidential election of 1928</a>, Hoover easily won the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_United_States_Republican_Party" title="History of the United States Republican Party">Republican nomination</a>, despite having no elected-office experience. Hoover is the most recent cabinet secretary to be elected President of the United States, as well as one of only two Presidents (along with <a href="/wiki/William_Howard_Taft" title="William Howard Taft">William Howard Taft</a>) elected without electoral experience or high military rank.</p>
<p>Hoover, a globally experienced engineer, believed strongly in the <a href="/wiki/Efficiency_Movement" title="Efficiency Movement">Efficiency Movement</a>, which held that the government and the economy were riddled with inefficiency and waste, and could be improved by experts who could identify the problems and solve them. He also believed in the importance of volunteerism and of the role of individuals in society and the economy. Hoover, who had made a small fortune in mining, was the first of two Presidents to redistribute his salary (President <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">Kennedy</a> was the other; he donated all his paychecks to charity).<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup> When the <a href="/wiki/Wall_Street_Crash_of_1929" title="Wall Street Crash of 1929">Wall Street Crash of 1929</a> struck less than eight months after he took office, Hoover tried to combat the ensuing <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a> with moderate government public works projects such as the <a href="/wiki/Hoover_Dam" title="Hoover Dam">Hoover Dam</a>. The record tariffs imbedded in the <a href="/wiki/Smoot-Hawley_Tariff" title="Smoot-Hawley Tariff" class="mw-redirect">Smoot-Hawley Tariff</a> and aggressive <a href="/wiki/Income_tax_in_the_United_States#History_of_federal_income_tax" title="Income tax in the United States">increases in the top tax bracket from 25% to 63%</a>, coupled with increases in <a href="/wiki/Corporate_tax_in_the_United_States" title="Corporate tax in the United States">corporate taxes</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup> yielded a "balanced budget" in 1933, but the economy plummeted simultaneously and unemployment rates rose to afflict one in four American workers. This downward spiral set the stage for Hoover's defeat in 1932 by Democrat <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a>, who promised a <a href="/wiki/New_Deal" title="New Deal">New Deal</a>. After Roosevelt assumed the Presidency in 1933, Hoover became a spokesman for opposition to the domestic and foreign policies of the New Deal. In 1947, President <a href="/wiki/Harry_S._Truman" title="Harry S. Truman">Harry S. Truman</a> appointed Hoover to head the <a href="/wiki/Hoover_Commission" title="Hoover Commission">Hoover Commission</a>, intended to foster greater efficiency throughout the federal bureaucracy. Most historians agree that Hoover's defeat in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1932" title="United States presidential election, 1932">1932 election</a> was caused primarily by the downward economic spiral, although his strong support for <a href="/wiki/Prohibition" title="Prohibition">prohibition</a> was also significant. Hoover is usually <a href="/wiki/Historical_rankings_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="Historical rankings of Presidents of the United States">ranked lower than average</a> among U.S. Presidents.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Family_background_and_early_life">Family background and early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Family background and early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:127px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Herbert_Hoover_in_1877.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Herbert_Hoover_in_1877.jpg/125px-Herbert_Hoover_in_1877.jpg" width="125" height="225" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Herbert_Hoover_in_1877.jpg/188px-Herbert_Hoover_in_1877.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Herbert_Hoover_in_1877.jpg/250px-Herbert_Hoover_in_1877.jpg 2x" data-file-width="334" data-file-height="600" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Herbert_Hoover_in_1877.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1877 Herbert Hoover Tintype</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Herbert Hoover was born on August 10, 1874, in <a href="/wiki/West_Branch,_Iowa" title="West Branch, Iowa">West Branch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iowa" title="Iowa">Iowa</a>, the first of his office born in that state and west of the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_River" title="Mississippi River">Mississippi River</a>. His father, Jesse Hoover (1849–1880), was a <a href="/wiki/Blacksmith" title="Blacksmith">blacksmith</a> and farm implement store owner, of German (Pfautz, Wehmeyer) and Swiss (Huber, Burkhart) ancestry. Jesse Hoover and his father Eli had moved to Iowa from <a href="/wiki/Ohio" title="Ohio">Ohio</a> twenty years previously.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup> Hoover's mother, Hulda Randall Minthorn (1849–1884), was born in <a href="/wiki/Norwich,_Ontario" title="Norwich, Ontario">Norwich</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ontario" title="Ontario">Ontario</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, and was of <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">English</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Irish</a> ancestry. Both of his parents were <a href="/wiki/Religious_Society_of_Friends" title="Religious Society of Friends" class="mw-redirect">Quakers</a>.</p>
<p>At about age two "Bertie", as he was then called, contracted the <a href="/wiki/Croup" title="Croup">croup</a>. He was so ill that he was momentarily thought to have died, until he was resuscitated by his uncle, John Minthorn.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup> As a child, he was often called by his father "my little stick in the mud", since he repeatedly was trapped in the mud while crossing an unpaved street.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> Hoover's family figured prominently in the town's public prayer life, due almost entirely to Hulda's role in her church.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> His father, noted by the local paper for his "pleasant, sunshiny disposition", died in 1880. After working to retire her husband's debts, retain their life insurance, and care for the children, his mother died in 1884, leaving Hoover (age nine), his older brother, and his younger sister as orphans.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> Fellow Quaker <a href="/wiki/Lawrie_Tatum" title="Lawrie Tatum">Lawrie Tatum</a> was appointed as Hoover's guardian.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Herbert_Hoover_birthplace.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Herbert_Hoover_birthplace.jpg/200px-Herbert_Hoover_birthplace.jpg" width="200" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Herbert_Hoover_birthplace.jpg/300px-Herbert_Hoover_birthplace.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Herbert_Hoover_birthplace.jpg/400px-Herbert_Hoover_birthplace.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Herbert_Hoover_birthplace.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Herbert Hoover birthplace cottage, West Branch, Iowa</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After a brief stay with one of his grandmothers in <a href="/wiki/Kingsley,_Iowa" title="Kingsley, Iowa">Kingsley, Iowa</a>, Hoover lived the next 18 months with his uncle Allen Hoover in West Branch. In November 1885, he went to <a href="/wiki/Newberg,_Oregon" title="Newberg, Oregon">Newberg, Oregon</a>, to live with his uncle Dr. John Minthorn, a physician and businessman whose own son had died the year before. The Minthorn household was considered cultured and educational, and imparted a strong work ethic.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> For two-and-a-half years, Hoover attended Friends Pacific Academy (now <a href="/wiki/George_Fox_University" title="George Fox University">George Fox University</a>), and then worked as an office assistant in his uncle's real estate office, the Oregon Land Company, in <a href="/wiki/Salem,_Oregon" title="Salem, Oregon">Salem, Oregon</a>. Though he did not attend high school, Hoover attended night school and learned bookkeeping, typing and mathematics.<sup id="cite_ref-e2_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-e2-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Hoover entered <a href="/wiki/Stanford_University" title="Stanford University">Stanford University</a> in 1891, its inaugural year, after failing all the entrance exams (except mathematics) and then being tutored for the summer in Palo Alto.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> The first-year students were not required to pay tuition.<sup id="cite_ref-e2_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-e2-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> Hoover claimed to be the very first student at Stanford, by virtue of having been the first person in the first class to sleep in the dormitory.<sup id="cite_ref-e1_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-e1-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> While at the university, he was the student manager of both the baseball and football teams and was a part of the inaugural <a href="/wiki/Big_Game_(American_football)" title="Big Game (American football)">Big Game</a> versus rival the <a href="/wiki/University_of_California,_Berkeley" title="University of California, Berkeley">University of California</a> and friend (CAL Manager) Herbert Lang.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> Only 10,000 tickets were printed for the inaugural game and 20,000 people showed up. Both Hoover and Lang had to find pots, bowls and any other available receptacles to collect admission fees.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup> Stanford won the game.<sup id="cite_ref-e1_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-e1-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> Hoover graduated in 1895 with a degree in geology.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> He earned his way through four years of college working various jobs on and off campus, including at the Arkansas and United States Geological Survey.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup> Throughout his tenure at Stanford, he was adamantly opposed to the fraternity system.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mining_engineer">Mining engineer</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Mining engineer">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Australia">Australia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Australia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Hoover went to <a href="/wiki/Western_Australia" title="Western Australia">Western Australia</a> in 1897 as an employee of Bewick, Moreing &amp; Co., a <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>-based gold mining company. His geological training and work experience were well suited for the firm's objectives.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup> He worked at gold mines in <a href="/wiki/Big_Bell,_Western_Australia" title="Big Bell, Western Australia">Big Bell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cue,_Western_Australia" title="Cue, Western Australia">Cue</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gwalia,_Western_Australia" title="Gwalia, Western Australia">Gwalia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Menzies,_Western_Australia" title="Menzies, Western Australia">Menzies</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Coolgardie,_Western_Australia" title="Coolgardie, Western Australia">Coolgardie</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup> Hoover first went to Coolgardie, then the center of the Western Australian goldfields, where he worked under Edward Hooper, a company partner. Conditions were harsh in these goldfields even though he got a $5,000 salary (equivalent to $100,000 today).<sup id="cite_ref-Hoover_biography_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hoover_biography-21"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup> In the Coolgardie and Murchison <a href="/wiki/Rangeland" title="Rangeland">rangelands</a> on the edge of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Victoria_Desert" title="Great Victoria Desert">Great Victoria Desert</a>, Hoover described the region as a land of "black flies, red dust and white heat".<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup> He served as a geologist and mining engineer while searching the Western Australian goldfields for investments. After being appointed as mine manager at the age of 23, he led a major program of expansion for the <a href="/wiki/Sons_of_Gwalia" title="Sons of Gwalia">Sons of Gwalia</a> <a href="/wiki/Gold_mine" title="Gold mine" class="mw-redirect">gold mine</a> at Gwalia, and brought in many <a href="/wiki/Italian_immigrants" title="Italian immigrants" class="mw-redirect">Italian immigrants</a> to cut costs and counter the <a href="/wiki/Australian_labour_movement" title="Australian labour movement">union militancy</a> of the Australian miners.<sup id="cite_ref-HG_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HG-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup> He believed "the rivalry between the Italians and the other men was of no small benefit."<sup id="cite_ref-HG_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HG-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup> He also described Italians as "fully 20 per cent superior"<sup id="cite_ref-HG_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HG-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup> to other miners.</p>
<p>During his time at Gwalia, Hoover first met Fleury James Lyster, a pioneering <a href="/wiki/Metallurgy" title="Metallurgy">metallurgist</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Fairweather_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fairweather-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup> In Western Australia friends called Hoover "H.C." or the old nickname "Hail Columbia".<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup> An open feud developed between Hoover and his boss Ernest Williams, with Hoover persuading four other mine managers to conspire against his rival. The firm's principals then offered Hoover a compelling promotion which would relocate him to China and also end the feud.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup> Hoover then began to take stock of his private life, including contemplating a separation from his Stanford sweetheart, <a href="/wiki/Lou_Henry_Hoover" title="Lou Henry Hoover">Lou Henry</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="China_and_other_global_operations">China and other global operations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: China and other global operations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Herbert_Hoover_in_1898.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Herbert_Hoover_in_1898.jpg/150px-Herbert_Hoover_in_1898.jpg" width="150" height="223" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Herbert_Hoover_in_1898.jpg/225px-Herbert_Hoover_in_1898.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/38/Herbert_Hoover_in_1898.jpg 2x" data-file-width="279" data-file-height="414" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Herbert_Hoover_in_1898.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Herbert Hoover, aged 23; taken in <a href="/wiki/Perth" title="Perth">Perth</a>, Western Australia in 1898</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Hoover promptly sent a cable of proposal to her, and subsequently married Lou Henry, in 1899. She had been an Episcopalian and became a Quaker. The Hoovers had two sons, <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Hoover_Jr." title="Herbert Hoover Jr." class="mw-redirect">Herbert Charles Hoover</a> (1903–1969) and <a href="/wiki/Allan_Hoover" title="Allan Hoover">Allan Henry Hoover</a> (1907–1993). The family subsequently went to China. Hoover worked as chief engineer for the Chinese Bureau of Mines, and as general manager for the Chinese Engineering and Mining Corporation.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup> Later he worked for Bewick, Moreing &amp; Co. as the company's lead engineer. Hoover and his wife learned <a href="/wiki/Standard_Mandarin" title="Standard Mandarin" class="mw-redirect">Mandarin Chinese</a> while he worked in China and used it during his tenure at the White House when they wanted to foil eavesdroppers.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup> Hoover made recommendations to improve the lot of the Chinese worker, seeking to end the practice of imposing long term servitude contracts and to institute reforms for workers based on merit.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Boxer_Rebellion" title="Boxer Rebellion">Boxer Rebellion</a> trapped the Hoovers in <a href="/wiki/Tianjin" title="Tianjin">Tianjin</a> in June 1900. For almost a month, the settlement was under fire, and both dedicated themselves to defense of their city. Hoover himself guided U.S. Marines around Tianjin during the battle, using his knowledge of the local terrain.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup> Mrs. Hoover meanwhile devoted her efforts at the various hospitals and even wielded, and willingly and accurately deployed, a .38-caliber pistol.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Hoover was made a partner in Bewick, Moreing &amp; Co. on December 18, 1901 and assumed responsibility for various Australian operations and investments.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> His initial compensation rose to $12,500 annually in addition to a 20% share of profits.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> The company eventually controlled at one point approximately 50% of gold production in Western Australia.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1901, Hoover no longer lived in Australia, but he visited the country in 1902, 1903, 1905, and 1907 as an overseas investor.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lou.henry.on.a.burro.at.acton.CA.1891.08.22.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/57/Lou.henry.on.a.burro.at.acton.CA.1891.08.22.jpg/300px-Lou.henry.on.a.burro.at.acton.CA.1891.08.22.jpg" width="300" height="239" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/57/Lou.henry.on.a.burro.at.acton.CA.1891.08.22.jpg/450px-Lou.henry.on.a.burro.at.acton.CA.1891.08.22.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/5/57/Lou.henry.on.a.burro.at.acton.CA.1891.08.22.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="478" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lou.henry.on.a.burro.at.acton.CA.1891.08.22.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Lou Henry, age 17, on a burro and rifle-ready at <a href="/wiki/Acton,_California" title="Acton, California">Acton, California</a> on August 22, 1891</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Hoover was also a director of Chinese Engineering and Mining Corporation (CEMC) when it became a supplier of immigrant labor from Southeast Asia for <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South African</a> mines.<sup id="cite_ref-Liggett_1930_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Liggett_1930-37"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup> The first shipment of almost 2,000 workers arrived in Durban from Qinhuangdao in July 1904. By 1906, the total number of immigrant workers increased to 50,000, almost entirely recruited and shipped by CEMC. When the living and working conditions of the laborers became known, public opposition to the scheme grew and questions were asked in the British Parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup> The scheme was abandoned in 1911.</p>
<p>In August–September 1905, he founded the <a href="/wiki/Zinc_Corporation" title="Zinc Corporation" class="mw-redirect">Zinc Corporation</a> (eventually part of the <a href="/wiki/Rio_Tinto_Group" title="Rio Tinto Group">Rio Tinto Group</a>) with <a href="/wiki/William_Baillieu" title="William Baillieu">William Baillieu</a> and others. The lead-silver ore produced at <a href="/wiki/Broken_Hill,_New_South_Wales" title="Broken Hill, New South Wales">Broken Hill, New South Wales</a> was rich in <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a>. But the zinc could not be recovered due to "the Sulphide Problem", and was left in the tailings that remained after the silver and lead was extracted.<sup id="cite_ref-Blainey_1968a_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blainey_1968a-39"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Zinc Corporation proposed to buy the tailings and extract the zinc by a new process. The <a href="/wiki/Froth_flotation" title="Froth flotation">froth flotation</a> process was then being developed at Broken Hill, although the Zinc Corporation struggled to apply it.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> Hoover came to Broken Hill in 1907. So did Australian engineer Jim Lyster, whose "Lyster Process",<sup id="cite_ref-Fairweather_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fairweather-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup> enabled the Zinc Corporation to operate the world's first selective or differential flotation plant, from September 1912.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> Hoover's brother, Theodore J. Hoover, also came to Broken Hill.</p>
<p>"Broken Hill was one of the dreariest places in the world at this time. It lay in the middle of the desert, was unbelievably hot in summer, had no fresh water, no vegetation, and mountains of tailings blew into every crack with every wisp of wind."<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup> Despite these miserable conditions, Hoover and his associates became suppliers to world industry of zinc and other vital base minerals.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sole_proprietor">Sole proprietor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Sole proprietor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HHoover.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/HHoover.jpg/170px-HHoover.jpg" width="170" height="231" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/HHoover.jpg/255px-HHoover.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/HHoover.jpg/340px-HHoover.jpg 2x" data-file-width="675" data-file-height="918" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HHoover.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Herbert Hoover in his 30s while a mining engineer.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1908, Hoover became an independent mining consultant, traveling worldwide until the outbreak of <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> in 1914. He left Bewick, Moreing &amp; Co and, setting out on his own, eventually ended up with investments on every continent and offices in <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco" title="San Francisco">San Francisco</a>, <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>, <a href="/wiki/St._Petersburg,_Florida" title="St. Petersburg, Florida">St. Petersburg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mandalay" title="Mandalay">Mandalay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Burma" title="Burma" class="mw-redirect">Burma</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup> He had his second successful venture with the British firm Burma Corporation, again producing silver, lead, and zinc in large quantities at the <a href="/wiki/Namtu" title="Namtu">Namtu</a> Bawdwin Mine, where he caught malaria in 1907.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup> While living in London, noting the American engineer's patriotic intensity, some British acquaintances referred to him as the "star-spangled Hoover". It recalled the nickname he had acquired in the Australian outback: "Hail Columbia" Hoover.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup> The Bawdwin mine ultimately became the chief source of Hoover's fortune.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In his spare time, Hoover wrote. His lectures at <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University" title="Columbia University">Columbia</a> and Stanford universities were published in 1909 as <i>Principles of Mining</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup> which became a standard textbook. Hoover and his wife also published their English translation of the 1556 mining classic <i><a href="/wiki/De_re_metallica" title="De re metallica">De re metallica</a></i> in 1912. This translation from the Latin of <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance" class="mw-redirect">Renaissance</a> author <a href="/wiki/Georgius_Agricola" title="Georgius Agricola">Georgius Agricola</a> is still the most important scholarly version and provides its historical context.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAgricola1912_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAgricola1912-48"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> It is still in print.</p>
<p>By 1914, Hoover was a wealthy man, with an estimated personal fortune of $4 million.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup> He was once quoted as saying "If a man has not made a million dollars by the time he is forty, he is not worth much".<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup> By 1914, Hoover stood eventually to obtain what he later described as "a large fortune from these Russian industries, probably more than is good for anybody".<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup> Sixty-six years after opening the mine in 1897, Hoover still had a partial share in the <a href="/wiki/Sons_of_Gwalia" title="Sons of Gwalia">Sons of Gwalia</a> mine when it finally closed in 1963, just one year before the former President's death in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a> in 1964. The successful mine had yielded $55m in gold and $10m in dividends for investors.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup> Herbert Hoover, acting as a main investor, financier, mining speculator, and organizer of men, played a major role in the important metallurgical developments that occurred in Broken Hill in the first decade of the twentieth century, developments that had a great impact on the mining and production of silver, lead, and zinc.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup> In later years Hoover thought of himself and his associates as "engineering doctors to sick concerns", hence his reputation as the "Doctor of sick mines".<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>After <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, Hoover's mining work in the <a href="/wiki/Kyshtym" title="Kyshtym">Kyshtym</a> area of Russia proved invaluable to American intelligence agencies. They had been unable to find detailed maps of the area, which contained the Soviets' first military <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a> production facility at <a href="/wiki/Mayak" title="Mayak">Mayak</a>, making knowledge of the area vital in the event of war with the Soviet Union. It was determined that Hoover had given extremely detailed maps of the area to Stanford University.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Humanitarian_work">Humanitarian work</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Humanitarian work">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relief_in_Europe_and_Belgium">Relief in Europe and Belgium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Relief in Europe and Belgium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:%22Wanted_Immediately._2,000,000_Garments_for_destitute_Men,_Women,_and_children_in_occupied_Northern_France_and..._-_NARA_-_512616.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/%22Wanted_Immediately._2%2C000%2C000_Garments_for_destitute_Men%2C_Women%2C_and_children_in_occupied_Northern_France_and..._-_NARA_-_512616.jpg/170px-%22Wanted_Immediately._2%2C000%2C000_Garments_for_destitute_Men%2C_Women%2C_and_children_in_occupied_Northern_France_and..._-_NARA_-_512616.jpg" width="170" height="239" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/%22Wanted_Immediately._2%2C000%2C000_Garments_for_destitute_Men%2C_Women%2C_and_children_in_occupied_Northern_France_and..._-_NARA_-_512616.jpg/255px-%22Wanted_Immediately._2%2C000%2C000_Garments_for_destitute_Men%2C_Women%2C_and_children_in_occupied_Northern_France_and..._-_NARA_-_512616.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/%22Wanted_Immediately._2%2C000%2C000_Garments_for_destitute_Men%2C_Women%2C_and_children_in_occupied_Northern_France_and..._-_NARA_-_512616.jpg/340px-%22Wanted_Immediately._2%2C000%2C000_Garments_for_destitute_Men%2C_Women%2C_and_children_in_occupied_Northern_France_and..._-_NARA_-_512616.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2131" data-file-height="3000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:%22Wanted_Immediately._2,000,000_Garments_for_destitute_Men,_Women,_and_children_in_occupied_Northern_France_and..._-_NARA_-_512616.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Poster for the <a href="/wiki/American_Relief_Administration" title="American Relief Administration">American Relief Administration</a> for Belgium directed by Hoover.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>When <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> began in August 1914, Hoover helped organize the return of around 120,000 Americans from Europe. He led 500 volunteers in distributing food, clothing, steamship tickets and cash.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup> "I did not realize it at the moment, but on August 3, 1914, my career was over forever. I was on the slippery road of public life."<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup> Hoover liked to say that the difference between dictatorship and democracy was simple: dictators organize from the top down, democracies from the bottom up.</p>
<p>When Belgium faced a food crisis after being invaded by Germany in 1914, Hoover undertook an unprecedented relief effort with the <a href="/wiki/Commission_for_Relief_in_Belgium" title="Commission for Relief in Belgium">Commission for Relief in Belgium</a> (CRB).<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup> As chairman of the CRB, Hoover worked with the leader of the Belgian <i><a href="/wiki/Comit%C3%A9_National_de_Secours_et_d%27Alimentation" title="Comité National de Secours et d'Alimentation">Comité National de Secours et d'Alimentation</a></i> (CNSA), <a href="/wiki/%C3%89mile_Francqui" title="Émile Francqui">Émile Francqui</a>, to feed the entire nation for the duration of the war. The CRB obtained and imported millions of tons of foodstuffs for the CNSA to distribute, and watched over the CNSA to make sure the German army didn't appropriate the food. The CRB became a veritable independent republic of relief, with its own flag, navy, factories, mills, and railroads. Private donations and government grants (78%) supplied an $11-million-a-month budget.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>For the next two years, Hoover worked 14-hour days from London, administering the distribution of over two million tons of food to nine million war victims. In an early form of <a href="/wiki/Shuttle_diplomacy" title="Shuttle diplomacy">shuttle diplomacy</a>, he crossed the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a> forty times to meet with German authorities and persuade them to allow food shipments, becoming an international hero. The Belgian city of <a href="/wiki/Leuven" title="Leuven">Leuven</a> named a prominent square <i>Hooverplein</i> after him. At its peak, Hoover's <a href="/wiki/American_Relief_Administration" title="American Relief Administration">American Relief Administration</a> (ARA) fed 10.5 million people daily. Great Britain grew reluctant to support the CRB, preferring instead to emphasize Germany's obligation to supply the relief; <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a>, whom Hoover intensely disliked, led a military faction that considered the Belgian relief effort "a positive military disaster".</p>
<p>During this time, Hoover made a strong impression on the American Ambassador to Great Britain, Walter Page. In a Memoranda dated December 30, 1916, Page wrote: Mr. Herbert C. Hoover, Chairman of the Commission for Relief in Belgium, would, if opportunity should offer, make a useful officer in the State Department. He is probably the only man living who has privately (i.e., without holding office) negotiated understandings with the British, French, German, Dutch, and Belgian governments. He personally knows and has had direct dealings with these governments, and his transactions with them have involved several hundred million dollars. He is a man of very considerable fortune—less than when the war began, for tins relief work has cost him much. He was approached on behalf of the British Government with the suggestion that if he would become a British subject the Government would be pleased to give him an important executive post and with the hint that if he succeeded a title might await him. His answer was: "I'll do what I can for you with pleasure; but I'll be damned if I'll give up my American citizenship—not on your life!" Within the last six months two large financial organizations, each independently, have offered him $100,000 a year to enter their service; and an industrial company offered him $100,000 "to start with." He declined them all. When the Belgian relief work recently struck a financial snag, Hoover by telegraph got the promise of a loan in the United States to the British and French governments for Belgian relief of $150,000,000&#160;! I do not know, but I think he would be glad to turn his European experience to the patriotic use of our government. He is forty-two years old, a graduate of Leland Stanford Jr. University.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="U.S._Food_Administration">U.S. Food Administration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: U.S. Food Administration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After the United States entered the war in April 1917, President Woodrow Wilson appointed Hoover to head the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Food_Administration" title="U.S. Food Administration" class="mw-redirect">U.S. Food Administration</a>, which was created under the Lever Food Control Act in 1917. This was a position he actively sought, though he later claimed it was thrust upon him. He was convinced from his Belgian work that centralization of authority was essential to any relief effort; he demanded, and got, great power albeit not as much as he sought.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup> Hoover believed "food will win the war"; and beginning on September 29, this slogan was introduced and put into frequent use.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>He carefully selected men to assist in the agency leadership – Alonzo Taylor (technical abilities), <a href="/wiki/Robert_A._Taft" title="Robert A. Taft">Robert Taft</a> (political associations), <a href="/wiki/Gifford_Pinchot" title="Gifford Pinchot">Gifford Pinchot</a> (agricultural influence) and Julius Barnes (business acumen).<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup> Hoover established set days for people to avoid eating specified foods and save them for soldiers' rations: meatless Mondays, wheatless Wednesdays, and "when in doubt, eat potatoes". This program helped reduce consumption of foodstuffs needed overseas and avoided rationing at home. It was dubbed "Hooverizing" by government publicists, in spite of Hoover's continual orders that publicity should not mention him by name. The agency employed a system of price controls and licensing requirements for suppliers to maximize production. Despite efforts to prevent it, some companies reaped great profits.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-war_relief">Post-war relief</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Post-war relief">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After the war, as a member of the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Economic_Council" title="Supreme Economic Council">Supreme Economic Council</a> and head of the <a href="/wiki/American_Relief_Administration" title="American Relief Administration">American Relief Administration</a>, Hoover organized shipments of food for millions of starving people in <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">Central Europe</a>. He used a newly formed Quaker organization, the <a href="/wiki/American_Friends_Service_Committee" title="American Friends Service Committee">American Friends Service Committee</a>, to carry out much of the logistical work in Europe.</p>
<p>Hoover provided aid to the defeated German nation after the war, as well as relief to <a href="/wiki/Famine" title="Famine">famine</a>-stricken <a href="/wiki/Bolshevik" title="Bolshevik" class="mw-redirect">Bolshevik</a>-controlled areas of <a href="/wiki/Russian_Soviet_Federative_Socialist_Republic" title="Russian Soviet Federative Socialist Republic">Russia</a> in 1921, despite the opposition of <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">Senator</a> <a href="/wiki/Henry_Cabot_Lodge" title="Henry Cabot Lodge">Henry Cabot Lodge</a> and other Republicans. When asked if he was not thus helping Bolshevism, Hoover retorted, "Twenty million people are starving. Whatever their politics, they shall be fed!". The <a href="/wiki/Russian_famine_of_1921" title="Russian famine of 1921">Russian famine</a> of 1921–22 claimed 6 million people.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup> In July 1922, Soviet author <a href="/wiki/Maxim_Gorky" title="Maxim Gorky">Maxim Gorky</a> wrote to Hoover:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Your help will enter history as a unique, gigantic achievement, worthy of the greatest glory, which will long remain in the memory of millions of Russians whom you have saved from death.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>At war's end, the <i><a href="/wiki/New_York_Times" title="New York Times" class="mw-redirect">New York Times</a></i> named Hoover one of the "Ten Most Important Living Americans". Hoover confronted a world of political possibilities when he returned home in 1919. <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic Party</a> leaders saw him as a potential Presidential candidate, and President Wilson privately preferred Hoover as his successor. "There could not be a finer one," asserted <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a>, then a rising star from New York. Hoover briefly considered becoming a Democrat, but he believed that 1920 would be a Republican year. Also, Hoover confessed that he could not run for a party whose only member in his boyhood home had been the town drunk.</p>
<p>Hoover realized that he was in a unique position to collect information about the Great War and its aftermath. In 1919 he established the <a href="/wiki/Hoover_Institution_Library_and_Archive" title="Hoover Institution Library and Archive" class="mw-redirect">Hoover War Collection</a> at Stanford University. He donated all the files of the Commission for Relief in Belgium, the U.S. Food Administration, and the American Relief Administration, and pledged $50,000 as an endowment. Scholars were sent to Europe to collect pamphlets, society publications, government documents, newspapers, posters, proclamations, and other ephemeral materials related to the war and the revolutions that followed it. The collection was later renamed the Hoover War Library and is now known as the <a href="/wiki/Hoover_Institution" title="Hoover Institution">Hoover Institution</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Secretary_of_Commerce">Secretary of Commerce</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Secretary of Commerce">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HooverCommerce1926.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/HooverCommerce1926.jpg/250px-HooverCommerce1926.jpg" width="250" height="162" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fa/HooverCommerce1926.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fa/HooverCommerce1926.jpg 2x" data-file-width="294" data-file-height="190" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HooverCommerce1926.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Assistants William McCracken (left) &amp; Walter Drake (right) with Secretary Hoover (center)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Hoover rejected the Democratic Party's overtures in 1920. He had been a registered Republican before the war, though he had supported Theodore Roosevelt's "Bull Moose" <a href="/wiki/Progressive_Party_(United_States,_1912)" title="Progressive Party (United States, 1912)">Progressive Party</a> in 1912. Now he declared himself a Republican and a candidate for the Presidency.</p>
<p>He placed his name on the ballot in the California state <a href="/wiki/Primary_election" title="Primary election">primary election</a>, where he nearly beat popular Senator <a href="/wiki/Hiram_Johnson" title="Hiram Johnson">Hiram Johnson</a>. But having lost in his home state, Hoover was not considered a serious contender at the convention. Even when it deadlocked for several ballots between Illinois Governor <a href="/wiki/Frank_Lowden" title="Frank Lowden" class="mw-redirect">Frank Lowden</a> and General <a href="/wiki/Leonard_Wood" title="Leonard Wood">Leonard Wood</a>, few delegates seriously considered Hoover as a compromise choice. Although he had personal misgivings about the capability of the nominee, <a href="/wiki/Warren_G._Harding" title="Warren G. Harding">Warren G. Harding</a>, Hoover publicly endorsed him and made two speeches for Harding.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hoover_and_Harding_at_baseball_game.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Hoover_and_Harding_at_baseball_game.jpg/200px-Hoover_and_Harding_at_baseball_game.jpg" width="200" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Hoover_and_Harding_at_baseball_game.jpg/300px-Hoover_and_Harding_at_baseball_game.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/09/Hoover_and_Harding_at_baseball_game.jpg 2x" data-file-width="330" data-file-height="237" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hoover_and_Harding_at_baseball_game.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hoover with President Harding at a baseball game in 1921.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After being elected, Harding rewarded Hoover for his support, offering to appoint him either <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_the_Interior" title="United States Secretary of the Interior">Secretary of the Interior</a> or <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_Commerce" title="United States Secretary of Commerce">Secretary of Commerce</a>. Hoover ultimately chose Commerce. Commerce had existed for just eight years, since the division of the earlier <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Commerce_and_Labor" title="United States Department of Commerce and Labor">Department of Commerce and Labor</a>. Commerce was considered a minor Cabinet post, with limited and vaguely defined responsibilities.</p>
<p>Hoover aimed to change that, envisioning the Commerce Department as the hub of the nation's growth and stability. From Harding he demanded, and received, authority to coordinate economic affairs throughout the government. He created many sub-departments and committees, overseeing and regulating everything from manufacturing statistics, the census and radio, to air travel. In some instances he "seized" control of responsibilities from other Cabinet departments when he deemed that they were not carrying out their responsibilities well. Hoover became one of the most visible men in the country, often overshadowing Presidents Harding and Coolidge. Washington wags referred to Hoover as "the Secretary of Commerce... and Under-Secretary of Everything Else!"</p>
<p>As secretary and later as President, Hoover revolutionized relations between business and government. Rejecting the adversarial stance of Theodore Roosevelt, William Howard Taft, and Woodrow Wilson, he sought to make the Commerce Department a powerful service organization, empowered to forge cooperative voluntary partnerships between government and business. This philosophy is often called "<a href="/wiki/Associationalism" title="Associationalism">associationalism</a>". Both the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Justice" title="United States Department of Justice">U.S. Department of Justice</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Trade_Commission" title="Federal Trade Commission">Federal Trade Commission</a> opposed Hoover's goals,<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup> but the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">U.S. Supreme Court</a> ruled in <i>Maple Flooring Manufacturers' Assn. v. United States 268 U.S. 563 (1925)</i> that Hoover's policies served the public interest by producing "fairer price levels" and "avoid[ing] waste."<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Many of Hoover's efforts as Commerce Secretary centered on eliminating waste and increasing efficiency in business and industry. This included reducing labor losses from trade disputes and seasonal fluctuations, reducing industrial losses from accident and injury, and reducing the amount of crude oil spilled during extraction and shipping. One major achievement was to promote product standardizations. He promoted international trade by opening overseas offices to advise businessmen. Hoover was especially eager to promote Hollywood films overseas.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHart1998_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHart1998-74"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>His "Own Your Own Home" campaign was a collaboration to promote ownership of single-family dwellings, with groups such as the Better Houses in America movement, the Architects' Small House Service Bureau, and the Home Modernizing Bureau. He worked with bankers and the <a href="/wiki/Savings_and_loan" title="Savings and loan" class="mw-redirect">savings and loan</a> industry to promote the new long-term home mortgage, which dramatically stimulated home construction.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>It has been suggested that Herbert Hoover was the best Secretary of Commerce in United States history.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup> Hoover was the last President to have held a full cabinet position.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Radio_conferences">Radio conferences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Radio conferences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:177px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HerbertClarkHoover.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/HerbertClarkHoover.jpg/175px-HerbertClarkHoover.jpg" width="175" height="231" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/HerbertClarkHoover.jpg/263px-HerbertClarkHoover.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/HerbertClarkHoover.jpg/350px-HerbertClarkHoover.jpg 2x" data-file-width="449" data-file-height="593" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HerbertClarkHoover.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Herbert Hoover listening to a <a href="/wiki/Radio_receiver" title="Radio receiver" class="mw-redirect">radio receiver</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Hoover's radio conferences played a key role in the early organization, development and regulation of radio broadcasting. Prior to the <a href="/wiki/Radio_Act_of_1927" title="Radio Act of 1927" class="mw-redirect">Radio Act of 1927</a>, the Secretary of Commerce was unable to deny radio licensing or reassign broadcast frequencies. With help from supporters Senator Dill and Representative White, Hoover brought the issue of radio control to the Senate floor. Hoover fought for more power to control the proliferation of licensed radio stations (which in 1927, stood at 732 stations). With help from Dill and White, Hoover promoted the Dill-White Bill which eventually would become the Radio Act of 1927. This act allowed the government to intervene and abolish radio stations that were deemed "non-useful" to the public. Hoover's attempts at regulating radio were not supported by all Congressmen, and he received much opposition from the Senate and from radio station owners. However, Hoover's contributions to regulate radio in its infancy heavily influenced the modern radio system.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Hoover contributed to major projects for navigation, irrigation of dry lands, electrical power, and flood control. As the new air transport industry developed, Hoover held a conference on aviation to promote codes and regulations. He became President of the American Child Health Organization, and he raised private funds to promote health education in schools and communities.</p>
<p>Although he continued to consider Harding ill-suited to be President, the two men nevertheless became friends. Hoover accompanied Harding on his final trip out West in 1923. It was Hoover who called for a specialist to tend to the ailing Chief Executive, and it was also Hoover who contacted the White House to inform them of the President's death. The Commerce Secretary headed the group of dignitaries accompanying Harding's body back to the capital.</p>
<p>By the end of Hoover's service as Secretary, he had raised the status of the Department of Commerce. This was reflected in its modern headquarters built during the Roosevelt Administration in the 1930s in the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Triangle" title="Federal Triangle">Federal Triangle</a> in Washington, D.C.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Traffic_conferences">Traffic conferences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Traffic conferences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As Commerce Secretary, Hoover also hosted two national conferences on street traffic, in 1924 and 1926 (a third convened in 1930, during Hoover's presidency). Collectively the meetings were called the National Conference on Street and Highway Safety. Hoover's chief objective was to address the growing casualty toll of traffic accidents, but the scope grew and soon embraced motor vehicle standards, rules of the road, and urban traffic control. He left the invited interest groups to negotiate agreements among themselves, which were then presented for adoption by states and localities. Because automotive trade associations were the best organized, many of the positions taken by the conferences reflected their interests. The conferences issued a model Uniform Vehicle Code for adoption by the states, and a Model Municipal Traffic Ordinance for adoption by cities. Both were widely influential, promoting greater uniformity between jurisdictions and tending to promote the automobile's priority in city streets.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mississippi_flood">Mississippi flood</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Mississippi flood">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Great_Mississippi_Flood_of_1927" title="Great Mississippi Flood of 1927">Great Mississippi Flood of 1927</a> broke the banks and <a href="/wiki/Levee" title="Levee">levees</a> of the lower Mississippi River in early 1927, resulting in flooding of millions of acres and leaving 1.5 million people displaced from their homes. Although such a disaster did not fall under the duties of the Commerce Department, the governors of six states along the Mississippi specifically asked for Herbert Hoover in the emergency. President Calvin Coolidge sent Hoover to mobilize state and local authorities, militia, army engineers, the Coast Guard, and the <a href="/wiki/American_Red_Cross" title="American Red Cross">American Red Cross</a>.</p>
<p>With a grant from the <a href="/wiki/Rockefeller_Foundation" title="Rockefeller Foundation">Rockefeller Foundation</a>, Hoover set up health units to work in the flooded regions for a year. These workers stamped out <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malaria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pellagra" title="Pellagra">pellagra</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Typhoid_fever" title="Typhoid fever">typhoid fever</a> from many areas. His work during the flood brought Herbert Hoover to the front page of newspapers almost everywhere, and he gained new accolades as a humanitarian. The great victory of his relief work, he stressed, was not that the government rushed in and provided all assistance; it was that much of the assistance available was provided by private citizens and organizations in response to his appeals. "I suppose I could have called in the Army to help", he said, "but why should I, when I only had to call upon Main Street."</p>
<p>The treatment of African-Americans during the disaster endangered Hoover's reputation as a humanitarian. Local officials brutalized black farmers and prevented them from leaving relief camps, aid intended for African-American <a href="/wiki/Sharecroppers" title="Sharecroppers" class="mw-redirect">sharecroppers</a> was often given instead to the landowners, and black men often were conscripted by locals into forced labor, sometimes at gun point.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup> Knowing the potential damage to his presidential hopes if this became public, Hoover struck a deal with <a href="/wiki/Robert_Russa_Moton" title="Robert Russa Moton">Robert Russa Moton</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-pbs_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pbs-81"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup> the prominent <a href="/wiki/African-American" title="African-American" class="mw-redirect">African-American</a> successor to <a href="/wiki/Booker_T._Washington" title="Booker T. Washington">Booker T. Washington</a> as president of the <a href="/wiki/Tuskegee_Institute" title="Tuskegee Institute" class="mw-redirect">Tuskegee Institute</a>. In exchange for keeping the sufferings of African-Americans quiet, Hoover promised unprecedented influence for African-Americans should he become president. Moton agreed, and following the accommodationist philosophy of Washington, he worked actively<sup id="cite_ref-pbs_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pbs-81"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup> to conceal the information from the media.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidential_election_of_1928">Presidential election of 1928</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Presidential election of 1928">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1928" title="United States presidential election, 1928">United States presidential election, 1928</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Republican_primaries">Republican primaries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Republican primaries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>When President Calvin Coolidge announced in August 1927 that he would not seek a second full term of office in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1928" title="United States presidential election, 1928">1928 presidential election</a>, Hoover became the leading Republican candidate, despite the fact Coolidge was lukewarm on Hoover, often deriding his ambitious and popular Commerce Secretary as "Wonder Boy".<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup> Coolidge had been reluctant to choose Hoover as his successor; on one occasion he remarked that "for six years that man has given me unsolicited advice—all of it bad. I was particularly offended by his comment to 'shit or get off the pot'."<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup> Even so, Coolidge had no desire to split the party by publicly opposing the popular Commerce Secretary's nomination.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Hoover's only real challenger was <a href="/wiki/Frank_Orren_Lowden" title="Frank Orren Lowden">Frank Orren Lowden</a>, a former governor of Illinois. Hoover received much favorable press coverage in the months leading up to the convention. Lowden's campaign manager complained that newspapers were full of "nothing but advertisements for Herbert Hoover and <a href="/wiki/Fletcher%27s_Castoria" title="Fletcher's Castoria" class="mw-redirect">Fletcher's Castoria</a>". Hoover's reputation, experience, and popularity coalesced to give him the Republican nomination on the first ballot.</p>
<p>The delegates considered nominating Vice President Charles Dawes to be Hoover's <a href="/wiki/Running_mate" title="Running mate">running mate</a>. But Coolidge (who hated Dawes) remarked that this would be "a personal affront" to him. The convention selected <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">Senator</a> <a href="/wiki/Charles_Curtis" title="Charles Curtis">Charles Curtis</a> of Kansas.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="General_election">General election</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: General election">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Hoover campaigned for efficiency and the Republican record of prosperity against Democrat <a href="/wiki/Alfred_E._Smith" title="Alfred E. Smith" class="mw-redirect">Alfred E. Smith</a>. Smith likewise was a proponent of efficiency earned as governor of New York. Both candidates were pro-business, and each promised to improve conditions for farmers, reform immigration laws, and maintain America's isolationist foreign policy. Where they differed was on the <a href="/wiki/Volstead_Act" title="Volstead Act">Volstead Act</a> which outlawed the sale of liquor and beer. Smith was a "wet" who called for its repeal, whereas Hoover gave limited support for prohibition, calling it an "experiment noble in purpose". His use of <i>"experiment"</i> suggested it was not permanent. While Smith won extra support among Catholics in the big cities Smith was the target of intense <a href="/wiki/Anti-Catholicism" title="Anti-Catholicism">anti-Catholicism</a> from some Protestant communities, especially as Southern Baptists and German Lutherans.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup> Overall the religious factor worked to the advantage of Hoover, although he took no part in it.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span>[</span>87<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Historians agree that Hoover's national reputation and the booming economy, combined with deep splits in the Democratic Party over religion and prohibition, guaranteed his landslide victory with 58% of the vote.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a></sup> Hoover's appeal to southern white voters succeeded in cracking the "<a href="/wiki/Solid_South" title="Solid South">Solid South</a>", winning the Democratic strongholds of Florida, North Carolina, Virginia, Texas and Tennessee; the Deep South continued to support Smith as the Democratic candidate. This was the first time that a Republican candidate for president had carried Texas. This outraged the black leadership, which largely broke from the Republican Party, and began seeking candidates who supported <a href="/wiki/Civil_rights" title="Civil rights" class="mw-redirect">civil rights</a> within the Democratic Party.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidency_.281929.E2.80.931933.29">Presidency (1929–1933)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Presidency (1929–1933)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Taft_Hebert_Hoover_Oath.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Taft_Hebert_Hoover_Oath.jpg/220px-Taft_Hebert_Hoover_Oath.jpg" width="220" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Taft_Hebert_Hoover_Oath.jpg/330px-Taft_Hebert_Hoover_Oath.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0a/Taft_Hebert_Hoover_Oath.jpg 2x" data-file-width="386" data-file-height="251" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Taft_Hebert_Hoover_Oath.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Inauguration of Hoover</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Inauguration_of_Herbert_Hoover" title="Inauguration of Herbert Hoover">Inauguration of Herbert Hoover</a></div>
<p>Hoover held a press conference on his first day in office, promising a "new phase of press relations".<sup id="cite_ref-american_chronicle_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-american_chronicle-90"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup> He asked the group of journalists to elect a committee to recommend improvements to the White House press conference. Hoover declined to use a spokesman, instead asking reporters to directly quote him and giving them handouts with his statements ahead of time. In his first 120 days in office, he held more regular and frequent press conferences than any other President, before or since. However, he changed his press policies after the 1929 stock market crash, screening reporters and greatly reducing his availability.<sup id="cite_ref-american_chronicle_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-american_chronicle-90"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Lou_Henry_Hoover" title="Lou Henry Hoover">Lou Henry Hoover</a> was an activist First Lady. She typified the new woman of the post–World War I era: intelligent, robust, and aware of multiple female possibilities.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>White House physician Admiral <a href="/wiki/Joel_T._Boone" title="Joel T. Boone" class="mw-redirect">Joel T. Boone</a> invented the sport <a href="/wiki/Hooverball" title="Hooverball">Hooverball</a> to keep Hoover fit while in the White House. Hooverball is a combination of <a href="/wiki/Volleyball" title="Volleyball">volleyball</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tennis" title="Tennis">tennis</a>, played with a <span class="nowrap">6 lb</span> medicine ball. Hoover and several staff members played it each morning, earning them the nickname <i><a href="/wiki/Medicine_Ball_Cabinet" title="Medicine Ball Cabinet">Medicine Ball Cabinet</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Hooverball_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hooverball-92"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Domestic_policies">Domestic policies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Domestic policies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:227px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Herbert_Hoover_as_the_new_President,_March_17,_1929.by_Oscar_Cesare.original_drawing.01.detail.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Herbert_Hoover_as_the_new_President%2C_March_17%2C_1929.by_Oscar_Cesare.original_drawing.01.detail.jpg/225px-Herbert_Hoover_as_the_new_President%2C_March_17%2C_1929.by_Oscar_Cesare.original_drawing.01.detail.jpg" width="225" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Herbert_Hoover_as_the_new_President%2C_March_17%2C_1929.by_Oscar_Cesare.original_drawing.01.detail.jpg/338px-Herbert_Hoover_as_the_new_President%2C_March_17%2C_1929.by_Oscar_Cesare.original_drawing.01.detail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Herbert_Hoover_as_the_new_President%2C_March_17%2C_1929.by_Oscar_Cesare.original_drawing.01.detail.jpg/450px-Herbert_Hoover_as_the_new_President%2C_March_17%2C_1929.by_Oscar_Cesare.original_drawing.01.detail.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2347" data-file-height="1760" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Herbert_Hoover_as_the_new_President,_March_17,_1929.by_Oscar_Cesare.original_drawing.01.detail.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Herbert Hoover as the new President of the United States; original drawing for an <a href="/wiki/Oscar_Cesare" title="Oscar Cesare">Oscar Cesare</a> <a href="/wiki/Political_cartoon" title="Political cartoon" class="mw-redirect">political cartoon</a>, March 17, 1929</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On poverty, Hoover said that "Given the chance to go forward with the policies of the last eight years, we shall soon with the help of God, be in sight of the day when poverty will be banished from this nation",<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup> and promised, "We in America today are nearer to the final triumph over poverty than ever before in the history of any land," but within months, the <a href="/wiki/Stock_Market_Crash_of_1929" title="Stock Market Crash of 1929" class="mw-redirect">Stock Market Crash of 1929</a> occurred, and the world's economy spiraled downward into the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Hoover entered office with a plan to reform the nation's regulatory system, believing that a federal bureaucracy should have limited regulation over a country's economic system.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup> A self-described <a href="/wiki/Progressivism" title="Progressivism">progressive</a> and <a href="/wiki/Reform_movement" title="Reform movement">reformer</a>, Hoover saw the presidency as a vehicle for improving the conditions of all Americans by encouraging public-private cooperation—what he termed "volunteerism". Hoover saw volunteerism as preferable to governmental coercion or intervention which he saw as opposed to the American ideals of individualism and self-reliance.<sup id="cite_ref-9iopl_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9iopl-96"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup> Long before he had entered politics, he had denounced <i><a href="/wiki/Laissez-faire" title="Laissez-faire">laissez-faire</a></i> thinking.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Hoover expanded <a href="/wiki/Civil_service" title="Civil service">civil service</a> coverage of Federal positions, canceled private oil leases on government lands, and by instructing the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Justice" title="United States Department of Justice">Justice Department</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Internal_Revenue_Service" title="Internal Revenue Service">Internal Revenue Service</a> to pursue gangsters for tax evasion, he enabled the prosecution of mobster <a href="/wiki/Al_Capone" title="Al Capone">Al Capone</a>. He appointed a commission that set aside 3,000,000 acres (12,000&#160;km²) of <a href="/wiki/National_park" title="National park">national parks</a> and 2,300,000 acres of national forests; advocated tax reduction for low-income Americans (not enacted); closed certain tax loopholes for the wealthy; doubled the number of veterans' hospital facilities; negotiated a treaty on <a href="/wiki/St._Lawrence_Seaway" title="St. Lawrence Seaway" class="mw-redirect">St. Lawrence Seaway</a> (which failed in the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Senate" title="U.S. Senate" class="mw-redirect">U.S. Senate</a>); wrote a Children's Charter that advocated protection of every child regardless of race or gender; created an antitrust division in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Justice" title="United States Department of Justice">Justice Department</a>; required air mail carriers to adopt stricter safety measures and improve service; proposed federal loans for <a href="/wiki/Urban_renewal" title="Urban renewal">urban slum clearances</a> (not enacted); organized the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Bureau_of_Prisons" title="Federal Bureau of Prisons">Federal Bureau of Prisons</a>; reorganized the <a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Indian_Affairs" title="Bureau of Indian Affairs">Bureau of Indian Affairs</a>; instituted prison reform; proposed a federal <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Education" title="United States Department of Education">Department of Education</a> (not enacted); advocated $50-per-month pensions for Americans over 65 (not enacted); chaired <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a> conferences on child health, protection, homebuilding and home-ownership; began construction of the <a href="/wiki/Boulder_Dam" title="Boulder Dam" class="mw-redirect">Boulder Dam</a> (later renamed Hoover Dam); and signed the <a href="/wiki/Norris%E2%80%93La_Guardia_Act" title="Norris–La Guardia Act">Norris–La Guardia Act</a> that limited judicial intervention in labor disputes.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
