<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_relations">Foreign relations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Foreign relations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On November 19, 1928, Hoover embarked on a seven-week <a href="/wiki/Goodwill_tour" title="Goodwill tour">goodwill tour</a> of several Latin American nations to outline his economic and trade policies to other nations in the <a href="/wiki/Western_Hemisphere" title="Western Hemisphere">Western Hemisphere</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Herbert_Hoover_and_King_Tut.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Herbert_Hoover_and_King_Tut.jpg/220px-Herbert_Hoover_and_King_Tut.jpg" width="220" height="305" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Herbert_Hoover_and_King_Tut.jpg/330px-Herbert_Hoover_and_King_Tut.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Herbert_Hoover_and_King_Tut.jpg/440px-Herbert_Hoover_and_King_Tut.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3623" data-file-height="5023" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Herbert_Hoover_and_King_Tut.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Herbert Hoover with his dog, King Tut.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Following the release in 1930 of the <a href="/wiki/Clark_Memorandum" title="Clark Memorandum">Clark Memorandum</a>, Hoover began formulating what would become Roosevelt's <a href="/wiki/Good_Neighbor_policy" title="Good Neighbor policy">Good Neighbor policy</a>. He began withdrawing American troops from Nicaragua and Haiti; he also proposed an arms embargo on Latin America and a one-third reduction of the world's naval power, which was called the Hoover Plan. The <a href="/wiki/Roosevelt_Corollary" title="Roosevelt Corollary">Roosevelt Corollary</a> ceased being part of U.S. foreign policy. In response to the Japanese invasion of <a href="/wiki/Manchuria" title="Manchuria">Manchuria</a>, he and Secretary of State <a href="/wiki/Henry_Stimson" title="Henry Stimson" class="mw-redirect">Henry Stimson</a> outlined the <a href="/wiki/Stimson_Doctrine" title="Stimson Doctrine">Hoover–Stimson Doctrine</a> which held that the United States would not recognize territories gained by force.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Hoover mediated between <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a> to solve a conflict on the sovereignty of <a href="/wiki/Arica,_Chile" title="Arica, Chile" class="mw-redirect">Arica</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tacna" title="Tacna">Tacna</a>, that in 1883 by the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Anc%C3%B3n" title="Treaty of Ancón">Treaty of Ancón</a> had been awarded to Chile for ten years, to be followed by a <a href="/wiki/Plebiscite" title="Plebiscite" class="mw-redirect">plebiscite</a> that had never happened. By the <a href="/wiki/Tacna%E2%80%93Arica_compromise" title="Tacna–Arica compromise" class="mw-redirect">Tacna–Arica compromise</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Lima" title="Treaty of Lima">Treaty of Lima</a> in 1929, Chile kept Arica, and Peru regained Tacna.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_rights">Civil rights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Civil rights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Hoover seldom mentioned civil rights while he was President. He believed that African-Americans and other races could improve themselves with education and wanted the races assimilated into white culture.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Hoover attempted to appoint <a href="/wiki/John_J._Parker" title="John J. Parker">John J. Parker</a> of North Carolina to the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court" title="Supreme Court" class="mw-redirect">Supreme Court</a> in 1930 to replace <a href="/wiki/Edward_Terry_Sanford" title="Edward Terry Sanford">Edward Sanford</a>. The <a href="/wiki/NAACP" title="NAACP" class="mw-redirect">NAACP</a> claimed that Parker had made many court decisions against African-Americans, and they fought the nomination. The NAACP was successful in gaining Senator <a href="/wiki/William_Borah" title="William Borah">William Borah</a>'s support and the nomination was defeated by two votes (39-41) in the Senate.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100"><span>[</span>100<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>First Lady <a href="/wiki/Lou_Henry_Hoover" title="Lou Henry Hoover">Lou Hoover</a> defied custom and invited the wife of Republican <a href="/wiki/Oscar_DePriest" title="Oscar DePriest" class="mw-redirect">Oscar DePriest</a>, the only African-American member in Congress, to <a href="/wiki/Jessie_DePriest_tea_at_the_White_House" title="Jessie DePriest tea at the White House">tea at the White House</a>. <a href="/wiki/Booker_T._Washington" title="Booker T. Washington">Booker T. Washington</a> was the previous African-American to have dined at the White House, with <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Theodore Roosevelt">Theodore Roosevelt</a> in 1901.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Charles_Curtis" title="Charles Curtis">Charles Curtis</a>, the nation's first <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">Native American</a> Vice President, was from the <a href="/wiki/Kaw_people" title="Kaw people">Kaw tribe</a> in Kansas.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102"><span>[</span>102<span>]</span></a></sup> Hoover's humanitarian and Quaker reputation, along with Curtis as a vice-president, gave special meaning to his Indian policies. His Quaker upbringing influenced his views that Native Americans needed to achieve economic self-sufficiency. As President, he appointed Charles J. Rhoads as commissioner of Indian affairs. Hoover supported Rhoads' commitment to Indian assimilation and sought to minimize the federal role in Indian affairs. His goal was to have Indians acting as individuals (not as tribes) and to assume the responsibilities of citizenship granted with the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Citizenship_Act_of_1924" title="Indian Citizenship Act of 1924" class="mw-redirect">Indian Citizenship Act of 1924</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103"><span>[</span>103<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Great_Depression">Great Depression</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Great Depression">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression_in_the_United_States" title="Great Depression in the United States">Great Depression in the United States</a></div>
<p>Hoover had long been a proponent of the concept that public-private cooperation was the way to achieve high long-term growth.<sup id="cite_ref-test_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-test-104"><span>[</span>104<span>]</span></a></sup> Hoover feared that too much government intervention would undermine long-term individuality and self-reliance, which he considered essential to the nation's future. Both his ideals and the economy were put to the test with the onset of the Great Depression.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105"><span>[</span>105<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Although many people at the time and for decades afterwards denounced Hoover for taking a hands-off ("<a href="/wiki/Laissez-faire" title="Laissez-faire">laissez-faire</a>") approach to the Depression,<sup id="cite_ref-The_Great_Depression_and_New_Deal_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Great_Depression_and_New_Deal-106"><span>[</span>106<span>]</span></a></sup> a few historians emphasize how active he actually was. Hoover said he rejected Treasury Secretary <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Mellon" title="Andrew Mellon">Andrew Mellon</a>'s suggested "leave-it-alone" approach,<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107"><span>[</span>107<span>]</span></a></sup> and called many business leaders to Washington to urge them not to lay off workers or cut wages.<sup id="cite_ref-memoirs_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-memoirs-108"><span>[</span>108<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Herbert_Hoover_and_Ted_Joslin.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Herbert_Hoover_and_Ted_Joslin.jpg/200px-Herbert_Hoover_and_Ted_Joslin.jpg" width="200" height="106" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Herbert_Hoover_and_Ted_Joslin.jpg/300px-Herbert_Hoover_and_Ted_Joslin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Herbert_Hoover_and_Ted_Joslin.jpg/400px-Herbert_Hoover_and_Ted_Joslin.jpg 2x" data-file-width="700" data-file-height="372" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Herbert_Hoover_and_Ted_Joslin.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Herbert Hoover in the Oval Office with Ted Joslin. 1932</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Libertarian economist <a href="/wiki/Murray_Rothbard" title="Murray Rothbard">Murray Rothbard</a> argues that Hoover was actually the initiator of what came to be the <a href="/wiki/New_Deal" title="New Deal">New Deal</a>. Hoover engaged in many unprecedented public works programs, including an increase in the Federal Buildings program of over $400 million and the establishment of the Division of Public Construction to spur public works planning. Hoover himself granted more subsidies to ship construction through the Federal Shipping Board and asked for a further $175 million appropriation for public works; this was followed in July 1930 with the expenditure of a giant $915 million public works program, including a Hoover Dam on the Colorado River.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109"><span>[</span>109<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup> In the spring of 1930, Hoover acquired from Congress an added $100 million to continue the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Farm_Board" title="Federal Farm Board">Federal Farm Board</a> lending and purchasing policies. At the end of 1929, the FFB established a national wool cooperative-the National Wool Marketing Corporation (NWMC) made up of 30 state associations. The Board also established an allied National Wool Credit Corporation to handle finances. A total of $31.5 million in loans for wool were made by the FFB, of which $12.5 million were permanently lost; these massive agricultural subsidies were a precedent for the later <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_Adjustment_Act" title="Agricultural Adjustment Act">Agricultural Adjustment Act</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111"><span>[</span>111<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112"><span>[</span>112<span>]</span></a></sup> Hoover also advocated strong labor regulation law, including the enactment of the Bacon-Davis Act, requiring a maximum <a href="/wiki/Eight-hour_day" title="Eight-hour day">eight-hour day</a> on construction of public buildings and the payment of at least the "prevailing wage" in the locality, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Norris-LaGuardia_Act" title="Norris-LaGuardia Act" class="mw-redirect">Norris-LaGuardia Act</a> in 1932. In the Banking sector, Hoover passed The Federal Home Loan Bank Act in July, 1932, establishing 12 district banks ruled by a Federal Home Loan Bank Board in a manner similar to the Federal Reserve System. $125 million capital was subscribed by the Treasury and this was subsequently shifted to the RFC. Hoover was also instrumental in passing the <a href="/wiki/Glass-Steagall" title="Glass-Steagall" class="mw-redirect">Glass-Steagall</a> Act of 1932, allowing for prime rediscounting at the Federal Reserve, allowing further inflation of credit and bank reserves.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113"><span>[</span>113<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Lee_Ohanian" title="Lee Ohanian" class="mw-redirect">Lee Ohanian</a>, from UCLA, argues that Hoover adopted pro-labor policies after the 1929 stock market crash that "accounted for close to two-thirds of the drop in the nation's gross domestic product over the two years that followed, causing what might otherwise have been a bad recession to slip into the Great Depression".<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114"><span>[</span>114<span>]</span></a></sup> This argument is at odds with the more <a href="/wiki/Keynesian" title="Keynesian" class="mw-redirect">Keynesian</a> view of the causes of the Depression, and has been challenged as revisionist by <a href="/wiki/J._Bradford_DeLong" title="J. Bradford DeLong">J. Bradford DeLong</a> of U.C. Berkeley.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115"><span>[</span>115<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Herbert_Hoover_and_Amelia_Earhart.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/43/Herbert_Hoover_and_Amelia_Earhart.jpg" width="150" height="274" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="150" data-file-height="274" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Herbert_Hoover_and_Amelia_Earhart.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
President Hoover with <a href="/wiki/Amelia_Earhart" title="Amelia Earhart">Amelia Earhart</a> in 1932.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Calls for greater government assistance increased as the U.S. economy continued to decline. He was also a firm believer in balanced budgets (as were most Democrats), and was unwilling to run a budget deficit to fund welfare programs.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116"><span>[</span>116<span>]</span></a></sup> However, Hoover did pursue many policies in an attempt to pull the country out of depression. In 1929 he authorized the <a href="/wiki/Mexican_Repatriation" title="Mexican Repatriation">Mexican Repatriation</a> program to help unemployed Mexican citizens return home. The program was largely a <a href="/wiki/Forced_migration" title="Forced migration">forced migration</a> of approximately 500,000 people to Mexico, and continued until 1937. In June 1930, over the objection of many economists, Congress approved and Hoover reluctantly signed into law the <a href="/wiki/Smoot%E2%80%93Hawley_Tariff_Act" title="Smoot–Hawley Tariff Act">Smoot–Hawley Tariff Act</a>. The legislation raised tariffs on thousands of imported items. The intent of the Act was to encourage the purchase of American-made products by increasing the cost of imported goods, while raising revenue for the federal government and protecting farmers. However, economic depression had spread worldwide, and Canada, France and other nations retaliated by raising tariffs on imports from the U.S. The result was to contract international trade, and worsen the Depression.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117"><span>[</span>117<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1931, Hoover issued the <a href="/wiki/Hoover_Moratorium" title="Hoover Moratorium">Hoover Moratorium</a>, calling for a one-year halt in <a href="/wiki/World_War_I_reparations" title="World War I reparations">reparation</a> payments by Germany to France and in the payment of Allied war debts to the United States. The plan was met with much opposition, especially from France, who saw significant losses to Germany during World War I. The Moratorium did little to ease economic declines. As the moratorium neared its expiration the following year, an attempt to find a permanent solution was made at the <a href="/wiki/Lausanne_Conference_of_1932" title="Lausanne Conference of 1932">Lausanne Conference of 1932</a>. A working compromise was never established, and by the start of World War II, reparations payments had stopped completely.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118"><span>[</span>118<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119"><span>[</span>119<span>]</span></a></sup> Hoover in 1931 urged the major banks in the country to form a consortium known as the National Credit Corporation (NCC).<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120"><span>[</span>120<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In the U.S. by 1932 unemployment had reached 24.9%,<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121"><span>[</span>121<span>]</span></a></sup> businesses defaulted on record numbers of loans, and more than 5,000 banks had failed.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a></sup> Hundreds of thousands of Americans found themselves homeless and began congregating in the numerous <a href="/wiki/Hooverville" title="Hooverville">Hoovervilles</a> (shanty towns) that sprang up in major cities.</p>
<p>Congress, desperate to increase federal revenue, enacted the <a href="/wiki/Revenue_Act_of_1932" title="Revenue Act of 1932">Revenue Act of 1932</a>, which was the largest peacetime tax increase in history.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123"><span>[</span>123<span>]</span></a></sup> The Act increased taxes across the board, so that top earners were taxed at 63% on their net income. The 1932 Act also increased the tax on the net income of corporations from 12% to 13.75%.</p>
<p>The final attempt of the Hoover Administration to rescue the economy occurred in 1932 with the passage of the <a href="/wiki/Emergency_Relief_and_Construction_Act" title="Emergency Relief and Construction Act">Emergency Relief and Construction Act</a>, which authorized funds for public works programs and the creation of the <a href="/wiki/Reconstruction_Finance_Corporation" title="Reconstruction Finance Corporation">Reconstruction Finance Corporation</a> (RFC). The RFC's initial goal was to provide government-secured loans to financial institutions, railroads and farmers. The RFC had minimal impact at the time, but was adopted by President Franklin D. Roosevelt and greatly expanded as part of his New Deal.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxes.2C_revenues.2C_and_deficits">Taxes, revenues, and deficits</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Taxes, revenues, and deficits">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Debt1929-50.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Debt1929-50.jpg/220px-Debt1929-50.jpg" width="220" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Debt1929-50.jpg/330px-Debt1929-50.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Debt1929-50.jpg/440px-Debt1929-50.jpg 2x" data-file-width="651" data-file-height="440" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Debt1929-50.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
National debt as a fraction of GNP up from 20% to 40% under Hoover. From <i>Historical Statistics US</i> (1976)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>To pay for these and other government programs and to make up for revenue lost due to the Depression, in addition to the <a href="/wiki/Revenue_Act_of_1932" title="Revenue Act of 1932">Revenue Act of 1932</a> Hoover agreed to roll back several tax cuts that his Administration had enacted on upper incomes. The <a href="/wiki/Estate_tax" title="Estate tax" class="mw-redirect">estate tax</a> was doubled and <a href="/wiki/Corporate_tax" title="Corporate tax">corporate taxes</a> were raised by almost 15%. Also, a "check tax" was included that placed a 2-cent tax (over 30 cents in today's economy) on all bank checks. Economists William D. Lastrapes and George Selgin,<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124"><span>[</span>124<span>]</span></a></sup> conclude that the check tax was "an important contributing factor to that period's severe monetary contraction". Hoover also encouraged Congress to investigate the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Stock_Exchange" title="New York Stock Exchange">New York Stock Exchange</a>, and this pressure resulted in various reforms.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:President_%26_Mrs_Hoover_in_Belvidere_IL_cph.3b12319.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/President_%26_Mrs_Hoover_in_Belvidere_IL_cph.3b12319.jpg/170px-President_%26_Mrs_Hoover_in_Belvidere_IL_cph.3b12319.jpg" width="170" height="202" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/President_%26_Mrs_Hoover_in_Belvidere_IL_cph.3b12319.jpg/255px-President_%26_Mrs_Hoover_in_Belvidere_IL_cph.3b12319.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/President_%26_Mrs_Hoover_in_Belvidere_IL_cph.3b12319.jpg/340px-President_%26_Mrs_Hoover_in_Belvidere_IL_cph.3b12319.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1182" data-file-height="1404" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:President_%26_Mrs_Hoover_in_Belvidere_IL_cph.3b12319.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Herbert and Lou Henry Hoover aboard a train in Illinois.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bonus_Army">Bonus Army</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Bonus Army">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bonus_Army" title="Bonus Army">Bonus Army</a></div>
<p>Thousands of World War I veterans and their families demonstrated and camped out in Washington, DC, during June 1932, calling for immediate payment of a bonus that had been promised by the <a href="/wiki/World_War_Adjusted_Compensation_Act" title="World War Adjusted Compensation Act">World War Adjusted Compensation Act</a> in 1924 for payment in 1945. Although offered money by <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_the_United_States" title="Congress of the United States" class="mw-redirect">Congress</a> to return home, some members of the "<a href="/wiki/Bonus_army" title="Bonus army" class="mw-redirect">Bonus army</a>" remained. Washington police attempted to remove the demonstrators from their camp, but they were outnumbered and unsuccessful. Shots were fired by the police in a futile attempt to attain order, and two protesters were killed while many officers were injured. Hoover sent U.S. Army forces led by General <a href="/wiki/Douglas_MacArthur" title="Douglas MacArthur">Douglas MacArthur</a> and helped by lower ranking officers <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">Dwight D. Eisenhower</a> and <a href="/wiki/George_S._Patton" title="George S. Patton">George S. Patton</a> to stop a march. MacArthur, believing he was fighting a communist revolution, chose to clear out the camp with military force. In the ensuing clash, hundreds of civilians were injured. Hoover had sent orders that the Army was not to move on the encampment, but MacArthur chose to ignore the command. Hoover was incensed, but refused to reprimand MacArthur. The entire incident was another devastating negative for Hoover in the <a href="/wiki/U.S._presidential_election,_1932" title="U.S. presidential election, 1932" class="mw-redirect">1932</a> election. That led New York governor and Democratic presidential candidate Franklin Roosevelt to declare of Hoover: "There is nothing inside the man but jelly!"</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Administration_and_cabinet">Administration and cabinet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Administration and cabinet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CabinetHoover.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/CabinetHoover.jpg/300px-CabinetHoover.jpg" width="300" height="190" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/CabinetHoover.jpg/450px-CabinetHoover.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/CabinetHoover.jpg/600px-CabinetHoover.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1299" data-file-height="823" /></a>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Supreme_Court_appointments">Supreme Court appointments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Supreme Court appointments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Hoover_Supreme_Court_candidates" title="Herbert Hoover Supreme Court candidates">Herbert Hoover Supreme Court candidates</a></div>
<p>Hoover appointed the following Justices to the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">Supreme Court of the United States</a>:<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125"><span>[</span>125<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<table class="infobox" cellspacing="5" style="width: auto; text-align:left; line-height: 1.2em; margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0em; float: none; clear: none;">
<tr>
<th colspan="3" style="line-height: 1.5em; font-size: 110%; background:#DCDCDC; text-align: center;">Supreme Court Appointments by President Herbert Hoover</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="col">Position</th>
<th scope="col">Name</th>
<th scope="col">Term</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="3" style="background: #000"></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td rowspan="1"><a href="/wiki/Chief_Justice_of_the_United_States" title="Chief Justice of the United States">Chief Justice</a></td>
<th style=""><a href="/wiki/Charles_Evans_Hughes" title="Charles Evans Hughes">Charles Evans Hughes</a></th>
<td>1930–1941</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="3" style="background: #000"></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td rowspan="2"><a href="/wiki/Associate_Justice_of_the_Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Associate Justice of the Supreme Court of the United States">Associate Justice</a></td>
<th style=""><a href="/wiki/Owen_Roberts" title="Owen Roberts">Owen Roberts</a></th>
<td>1930–1945</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th style=""><a href="/wiki/Benjamin_N._Cardozo" title="Benjamin N. Cardozo">Benjamin N. Cardozo</a></th>
<td>1932–1938</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Hoover broke party lines to appoint the Democrat Cardozo. He explained that he "was one of the ancient believers that the Supreme Court should have a strong minority of the opposition's party and that all appointments should be made from experienced jurists. When the vacancy came... [Hoover] canvassed all the possible Democratic jurists and immediately concluded that Justice Cardozo was the right man and appointed him."<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126"><span>[</span>126<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="1932_re-election_campaign">1932 re-election campaign</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: 1932 re-election campaign">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:179px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hoover_Campaign.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2e/Hoover_Campaign.jpg" width="177" height="215" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="177" data-file-height="215" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hoover_Campaign.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hoover addresses a large crowd in his 1932 campaign.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1932" title="United States presidential election, 1932">United States presidential election, 1932</a></div>
<p>Although Hoover had come to detest the presidency, he agreed to run again in 1932, not only as a matter of pride, but also because he feared that no other likely Republican candidate would deal with the depression without resorting to what Hoover considered dangerously radical measures.</p>
<p>Hoover was nominated by the Republicans for a second term. He had originally planned to make only one or two major speeches, and to leave the rest of the campaigning to proxies, but when polls showed the entire Republican ticket facing a resounding defeat at the polls, Hoover agreed to an expanded schedule of public addresses. In his nine major radio addresses Hoover primarily defended his administration and his philosophy. The apologetic approach did not allow Hoover to refute Democratic nominee Franklin Roosevelt's charge that he was personally responsible for the depression.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127"><span>[</span>127<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In his campaign trips around the country, Hoover was faced with perhaps the most hostile crowds of any sitting president. Besides having his train and motorcades pelted with eggs and rotten fruit, he was often heckled while speaking, and on several occasions, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secret_Service" title="United States Secret Service">Secret Service</a> halted attempts to kill Hoover by disgruntled citizens, including capturing one man nearing Hoover carrying sticks of dynamite, and another already having removed several spikes from the rails in front of the President's train.<sup id="cite_ref-time_2008_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-time_2008-128"><span>[</span>128<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Franklin D. Roosevelt blasted Hoover for spending and taxing too much, increasing national debt, raising tariffs and blocking trade, as well as placing millions on the government dole. Roosevelt attacked Hoover for "reckless and extravagant" spending, of thinking "that we ought to center control of everything in Washington as rapidly as possible".<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129"><span>[</span>129<span>]</span></a></sup> Roosevelt's running mate, <a href="/wiki/John_Nance_Garner" title="John Nance Garner">John Nance Garner</a>, accused the Republican of "leading the country down the path of socialism".<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130"><span>[</span>130<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Osro_Cobb" title="Osro Cobb">Osro Cobb</a>, a leader of the Republican Party in Arkansas who became politically and personally close to Hoover, recalls:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>President Hoover had become convinced that the Democrats deliberately were destroying the economy of the country and erecting roadblocks against every measure he offered to the Congress to restore balance to the economy ... all for the purpose of winning an election. Just a few weeks before the 1932 election, we were standing near a window in the Oval Office. His cigar was frayed and out, and he was in deep thought and obviously troubled. He turned aside and said that he had accepted a speaking engagement in Des Moines, Iowa, in three days and that the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Secret_Service" title="U.S. Secret Service" class="mw-redirect">U.S. Secret Service</a> had warned him that it had uncovered evidence of plots by radical elements to assassinate him if he kept it. Turmoil and uncertainty prevailed in the country, but there was absolutely no fear in his expression; to the contrary, there appeared to be an abundance of personal courage. Frankly, my heart went out to him, but I pointed out that fate and destiny played a part in the lives of all presidents and that I felt all possible precautions should be taken to protect him but that he should appear and make one of the greatest speeches of his administration. He smiled and said, "Osro, that's what I have already decided to do. Your concurrence is comforting." ...<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131"><span>[</span>131<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Despite the late campaign endeavors, Hoover sustained a large defeat in the election, having procured only 39.7 percent of the popular vote to Roosevelt's 57.4 percent. Hoover's popular vote was reduced by 26 percentage points from his result in the 1928 election. In the <a href="/wiki/Electoral_College_(United_States)" title="Electoral College (United States)">electoral college</a> he carried only Pennsylvania, Delaware, and four other Northeastern states to lose 59–472. The Democrats extended their control over the U.S. House and gained control of the U.S. Senate.</p>
<p>After the election, Hoover requested that Roosevelt retain the <a href="/wiki/Gold_standard" title="Gold standard">Gold standard</a> as the basis of the US currency, and in effect, continue many of the Hoover Administration's economic policies. Roosevelt refused.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132"><span>[</span>132<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-presidency">Post-presidency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Post-presidency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:FDR_Inauguration_1933.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/FDR_Inauguration_1933.jpg/180px-FDR_Inauguration_1933.jpg" width="180" height="225" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/FDR_Inauguration_1933.jpg/270px-FDR_Inauguration_1933.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/FDR_Inauguration_1933.jpg/360px-FDR_Inauguration_1933.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2790" data-file-height="3480" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:FDR_Inauguration_1933.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hoover with Franklin D. Roosevelt, March 4, 1933</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Hoover departed from Washington in March 1933 with some bitterness, disappointed both that he had been repudiated by the voters and unappreciated for his best efforts. The Hoovers went first to New York City, where they stayed for a while in the <a href="/wiki/Waldorf-Astoria_Hotel" title="Waldorf-Astoria Hotel" class="mw-redirect">Waldorf-Astoria Hotel</a>. Later that spring, they returned to California to their Stanford residence. Hoover enjoyed returning to the men's clubs that he had long been involved with, including the <a href="/wiki/Bohemian_Club" title="Bohemian Club">Bohemian Club</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Pacific-Union_Club" title="Pacific-Union Club">Pacific-Union Club</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/University_Club_of_San_Francisco" title="University Club of San Francisco">University Club</a> in San Francisco.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133"><span>[</span>133<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Hoover liked to drive his car, accompanied by his wife or a friend (former Presidents did not get <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secret_Service" title="United States Secret Service">Secret Service</a> protection until the 1960s), and drive on wandering journeys, visiting Western mining camps or small towns where he often went unrecognized, or heading up to the mountains, or deep into the woods, to go fishing in relative solitude. A year before his death, his own fishing days behind him, he published <i>Fishing For Fun—And To Wash Your Soul</i>, the last of more than sixteen books in his lifetime.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:197px;"><a href="/wiki/File:President_Herbert_Hoover_with_a_day%27s_catch._(no_original_caption)_-_NARA_-_513114.tif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/President_Herbert_Hoover_with_a_day%27s_catch._%28no_original_caption%29_-_NARA_-_513114.tif/lossy-page1-195px-President_Herbert_Hoover_with_a_day%27s_catch._%28no_original_caption%29_-_NARA_-_513114.tif.jpg" width="195" height="136" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/President_Herbert_Hoover_with_a_day%27s_catch._%28no_original_caption%29_-_NARA_-_513114.tif/lossy-page1-293px-President_Herbert_Hoover_with_a_day%27s_catch._%28no_original_caption%29_-_NARA_-_513114.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/President_Herbert_Hoover_with_a_day%27s_catch._%28no_original_caption%29_-_NARA_-_513114.tif/lossy-page1-390px-President_Herbert_Hoover_with_a_day%27s_catch._%28no_original_caption%29_-_NARA_-_513114.tif.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="2098" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:President_Herbert_Hoover_with_a_day%27s_catch._(no_original_caption)_-_NARA_-_513114.tif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hoover fishing in his home state of Iowa.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Although many of his friends and supporters called upon Hoover to speak out against FDR's New Deal and to assume his place as the voice of the "loyal opposition", he refused to do so for many years after leaving the White House, and he largely kept himself out of the public spotlight until late in 1934. However, that did not stop rumors springing up about him, often fanned by Democratic politicians who found the former President to be a convenient <a href="/wiki/Scapegoat" title="Scapegoat">scapegoat</a>.</p>
<p>The relationship between Hoover and Roosevelt was one of the most severely strained in Presidential history. Hoover had little good to say about his successor. FDR, in turn, supposedly engaged in various petty official acts aimed at his predecessor, ranging from dropping him from the White House birthday greetings message list to having Hoover's name struck from the Hoover Dam on the Colorado River, which would officially be known only as Boulder Dam for many years to come.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Books_fishing_for_fun.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/2c/Books_fishing_for_fun.jpg/250px-Books_fishing_for_fun.jpg" width="250" height="179" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/2c/Books_fishing_for_fun.jpg/375px-Books_fishing_for_fun.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/2c/Books_fishing_for_fun.jpg/500px-Books_fishing_for_fun.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="430" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Books_fishing_for_fun.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hoover's book, <i>Fishing For Fun – And To Wash Your Soul</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1936" title="United States presidential election, 1936">1936</a>, Hoover entertained hopes of receiving the Republican presidential nomination again, and thus facing Roosevelt in a rematch. However, although he retained strong support among some delegates, there was never much hope of his being selected. He publicly endorsed the nominee, <a href="/wiki/Kansas" title="Kansas">Kansas</a> Governor <a href="/wiki/Alf_Landon" title="Alf Landon">Alf Landon</a>. But Hoover might as well have been the nominee, since the Democrats virtually ignored Landon, and they ran against the former President himself, constantly attacking him in speeches and warning that a Landon victory would put Hoover back in the White House as the secret power "behind the throne". Roosevelt won 46 of the 48 states, burying Landon in the Electoral College, and the Republican Party in Congress in another landslide.</p>
<p>Although Hoover's reputation was at its low point, circumstances began to rehabilitate his name and restore him to prominence. Roosevelt overreached on his <a href="/wiki/Judiciary_Reorganization_Bill_of_1937" title="Judiciary Reorganization Bill of 1937" class="mw-redirect">Supreme Court packing</a> plan, and a further financial recession in 1937 and 1938 tarnished his image of invincibility.</p>
<p>In 1939, former President Herbert Hoover became the first Honorary Chairman of <a href="/wiki/Tolstoy_Foundation" title="Tolstoy Foundation">Tolstoy Foundation</a> in <a href="/wiki/Valley_Cottage,_New_York" title="Valley Cottage, New York">Valley Cottage, New York</a>, served in this capacity until his death in 1964.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134"><span>[</span>134<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>By 1940, Hoover was again being spoken of as the possible nominee of the party in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1940" title="United States presidential election, 1940">presidential election</a>. Although he trailed in the polls behind <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Dewey" title="Thomas Dewey" class="mw-redirect">Thomas Dewey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Vandenberg" title="Arthur Vandenberg" class="mw-redirect">Arthur Vandenberg</a>, and his own former protege, <a href="/wiki/Robert_A._Taft" title="Robert A. Taft">Robert A. Taft</a>, he still had considerable first-ballot delegate strength, and it was believed that if the convention deadlocked between the leading candidates, the party might turn to him as its compromise. However, the convention nominated the utility company president <a href="/wiki/Wendell_Willkie" title="Wendell Willkie">Wendell Willkie</a>, who had supported Roosevelt in 1932 but turned against him after the creation of the <a href="/wiki/Tennessee_Valley_Authority" title="Tennessee Valley Authority">Tennessee Valley Authority</a> forced him to sell his company. Hoover dutifully supported Willkie, although he despaired that the nominee endorsed a platform that, to Hoover, was little more than the New Deal in all but name.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Road_to_war_and_World_War_II">Road to war and World War II</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Road to war and World War II">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Hoover visited 10 European countries in March 1938, the month of <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Anschluss" title="Anschluss">Anschluss</a></i> of <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a>, and stated "I do not believe a widespread war is at all probable in the near future. There is a general realization everywhere ... that civilization as we know it cannot survive another great war."<sup id="cite_ref-life1938032819_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-life1938032819-135"><span>[</span>135<span>]</span></a></sup> Like many, he initially believed that the European Allies would be able to contain Germany, and that <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Japan" title="Empire of Japan">Imperial Japan</a> would not attack American interests in the Pacific.</p>
<p>Unlike Roosevelt's administration, Hoover was a vocal supporter of providing relief to countries in <a href="/wiki/Nazi-occupied_Europe" title="Nazi-occupied Europe" class="mw-redirect">Nazi-occupied Europe</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Walch2003_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Walch2003-136"><span>[</span>136<span>]</span></a></sup> He was instrumental in creating the <a href="/wiki/Commission_for_Polish_Relief" title="Commission for Polish Relief">Commission for Polish Relief</a> and <a href="/wiki/Finnish_Relief_Fund" title="Finnish Relief Fund">Finnish Relief Fund</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137"><span>[</span>137<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hoover1964_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hoover1964-138"><span>[</span>138<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>When the Germans overran France and then had Britain held in a stalemate, many Americans saw Britain as on the verge of collapse. Nonetheless, Hoover declared that it would be folly for the United States to declare war on Germany and to rush to save the United Kingdom. Rather, he held, it was far wiser for this nation to devote itself to building up its own defenses, and to wash its hands of the mess in Europe. He called for a "Fortress America" concept, in which the United States, protected on the East and on the West by vast oceans patrolled by its Navy and its Air Corps (the <a href="/wiki/USAAF" title="USAAF" class="mw-redirect">USAAF</a>), could adequately repel any attack on the Americas.</p>
<p>During a radio broadcast on June 29, 1941, one week after the Nazi invasion of the Soviet Union, Hoover disparaged any "tacit alliance" between the U.S. and the USSR by saying:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>If we go further and join the war and we win, then we have won for Stalin the grip of communism on Russia... Again I say, if we join the war and Stalin wins, we have aided him to impose more communism on Europe and the world. At least we could not with such a bedfellow say to our sons that by making the supreme sacrifice, they are restoring freedom to the world. War alongside Stalin to impose freedom is more than a travesty. It is a tragedy.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139"><span>[</span>139<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>When the United States entered the war following the December 7, 1941, Japanese <a href="/wiki/Attack_on_Pearl_Harbor" title="Attack on Pearl Harbor">attack on Pearl Harbor</a>, Hoover swept aside all feelings of neutrality and called for total victory. He offered himself to the government in any capacity necessary, but the Roosevelt Administration did not call upon him to serve.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Post.E2.80.93World_War_II">Post–World War II</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Post–World War II">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hoover_men_Allan_Herbert_Sr_Andrew_1950.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Hoover_men_Allan_Herbert_Sr_Andrew_1950.jpg/220px-Hoover_men_Allan_Herbert_Sr_Andrew_1950.jpg" width="220" height="283" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Hoover_men_Allan_Herbert_Sr_Andrew_1950.jpg/330px-Hoover_men_Allan_Herbert_Sr_Andrew_1950.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Hoover_men_Allan_Herbert_Sr_Andrew_1950.jpg/440px-Hoover_men_Allan_Herbert_Sr_Andrew_1950.jpg 2x" data-file-width="580" data-file-height="745" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hoover_men_Allan_Herbert_Sr_Andrew_1950.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Herbert Hoover with his son <a href="/wiki/Allan_Hoover" title="Allan Hoover">Allan</a> <i>(left)</i> and his grandson Andrew <i>(above)</i>, 1950</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Following <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, Hoover became friends with President <a href="/wiki/Harry_S._Truman" title="Harry S. Truman">Harry S. Truman</a>. Hoover joked that they were for many years the sole members of the "trade union" of former Presidents (since Calvin Coolidge and Roosevelt were dead already). Because of Hoover's previous experience with Germany at the end of World War I, in 1946 President Truman selected the former president to tour Germany to ascertain the food status of the occupied nation. Hoover toured what was to become West Germany in <a href="/wiki/Hermann_G%C3%B6ring" title="Hermann Göring">Hermann Göring</a>'s old train coach and produced <a href="/wiki/The_President%27s_Economic_Mission_to_Germany_and_Austria" title="The President's Economic Mission to Germany and Austria">a number of reports</a> critical of U.S. occupation policy. The economy of Germany had "sunk to the lowest level in a hundred years".<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140"><span>[</span>140<span>]</span></a></sup> He stated in one report:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>There is the illusion that the New Germany left after the <a href="/wiki/Historical_Eastern_Germany#Potsdam_Conference" title="Historical Eastern Germany" class="mw-redirect">annexations</a> can be reduced to a "<a href="/wiki/Morgenthau_Plan" title="Morgenthau Plan">pastoral state</a>". It cannot be done unless we <a href="/wiki/Genocide" title="Genocide">exterminate</a> or move 25,000,000 people out of it.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141"><span>[</span>141<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>On Hoover's initiative, a school meals program in the American and British occupation zones of Germany was begun on April 14, 1947. The program served 3,500,000 children aged six through 18. A total of 40,000 tons of American food was provided during the <i>Hooverspeisung</i> (Hoover meals).</p>
<p>In 1947, President Harry S. Truman appointed Hoover to a commission, which elected him chairman, to reorganize the executive departments. This became known as the <a href="/wiki/Hoover_Commission" title="Hoover Commission">Hoover Commission</a>. He was appointed chairman of a similar commission by President <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">Dwight D. Eisenhower</a> in 1953. Both found numerous inefficiencies and ways to reduce waste. The government enacted most of the recommendations that the two commissions had made: 71% of the first commission's and 64% of the second commission's.</p>
<p>Throughout the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>, Hoover, always an opponent of <a href="/wiki/Marxism" title="Marxism">Marxism</a>, became even more outspokenly <a href="/wiki/Anti-Communist" title="Anti-Communist" class="mw-redirect">anti-Communist</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142"><span>[</span>142<span>]</span></a></sup> However, he vehemently opposed American involvement in the <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">Korean War</a>, saying that "To commit the sparse ground forces of the non-communist nations into a land war against this communist land mass [in Asia] would be a war without victory, a war without a successful political terminal... that would be the graveyard of millions of American boys and the exhaustion of the United States."<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143"><span>[</span>143<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hhoover.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Hhoover.jpeg/220px-Hhoover.jpeg" width="220" height="278" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Hhoover.jpeg/330px-Hhoover.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Hhoover.jpeg/440px-Hhoover.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="2376" data-file-height="3000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hhoover.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hoover's official <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a> portrait painted by Elmer Wesley Greene.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Despite his advancing years, Hoover continued to work nearly full-time both on writing (among his literary works is <i>The Ordeal of Woodrow Wilson</i>, a bestseller, and the first time one former President had ever written a biography about another), as well as overseeing the Hoover Institution at <a href="/wiki/Stanford_University" title="Stanford University">Stanford University</a>, which housed not only his own professional papers, but also those of a number of other former high ranking governmental and military servants. He also threw himself into fund-raising for the Boys Clubs (now the <a href="/wiki/Boys_%26_Girls_Clubs_of_America" title="Boys &amp; Girls Clubs of America">Boys &amp; Girls Clubs of America</a>), which became his pet charity.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Final_years_and_death">Final years and death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Final years and death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>From Coolidge's death in 1933 to <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">Dwight D. Eisenhower</a>'s last day of serving the presidency in 1961, Hoover had been the only living Republican former president. In 1960, Hoover appeared at his final Republican National Convention. Since the 1948 convention, he had been feted as the guest of "farewell" ceremonies (the unspoken assumption being that the aging former President might not survive until the next convention). Joking to the delegates, he said, "Apparently, my last three good-byes didn't take." Although he lived to see the 1964 convention, ill health prevented him from attending. The Presidential nominee <a href="/wiki/Barry_Goldwater" title="Barry Goldwater">Barry Goldwater</a> acknowledged Hoover's absence in his acceptance speech. In 1962, Hoover had a malignant intestinal tumor removed. Ten months later he had severe gastrointestinal bleeding and seemed terminally ill and frail, but his mind was clear and he maintained a great deal of correspondence. Although the illness would get worse over time, he refused to be hospitalized.</p>
<p>Hoover died following massive <a href="/wiki/Internal_bleeding" title="Internal bleeding">internal bleeding</a> at the age of 90 in his New York City suite at 11:35&#160;a.m. on October 20, 1964,<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144"><span>[</span>144<span>]</span></a></sup> 31 years, seven months, and sixteen days after leaving office. At the time of his death, he had the longest retirement of any President. Former President <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">Jimmy Carter</a> surpassed the length of Hoover's retirement on September 7, 2012. At the time of Hoover's death he was the second longest-lived president after <a href="/wiki/John_Adams" title="John Adams">John Adams</a>; both were since surpassed by <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Ford" title="Gerald Ford">Gerald Ford</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_H._W._Bush" title="George H. W. Bush">George H. W. Bush</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">Jimmy Carter</a>. He had outlived by 20 years his wife, Lou Henry Hoover, who had died in 1944, and he was the last living member of the <a href="/wiki/Calvin_Coolidge#Administration_and_Cabinet" title="Calvin Coolidge">Coolidge</a> administration. He also outlived both his successor <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Eleanor_Roosevelt" title="Eleanor Roosevelt">Eleanor Roosevelt</a> who died in 1945 and 1962, respectively. Hoover was the last President to be the only living ex-President until the death of <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson" title="Lyndon B. Johnson">Lyndon B. Johnson</a> in 1973, thus making <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Richard Nixon</a> the most recent President to be the only living ex-President. Hoover's time as only living President was also the shortest in history, at one day less than two months.</p>
<p>By the time of his death, he had rehabilitated his image.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145"><span>[</span>145<span>]</span></a></sup> His birthplace in Iowa and an Oregon home where he lived as a child, became National Landmarks during his lifetime. His <a href="/wiki/Rapidan_Camp" title="Rapidan Camp">Rapidan fishing camp</a> in Virginia, which he had donated to the government in 1933, is now a National Historic Landmark within the <a href="/wiki/Shenandoah_National_Park" title="Shenandoah National Park">Shenandoah National Park</a>. Hoover and his wife are buried at the <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Hoover_Presidential_Library_and_Museum" title="Herbert Hoover Presidential Library and Museum">Herbert Hoover Presidential Library and Museum</a> in <a href="/wiki/West_Branch,_Iowa" title="West Branch, Iowa">West Branch, Iowa</a>. Hoover was honored with a <a href="/wiki/State_funeral" title="State funeral">state funeral</a>, the last of three in a span of 12 months, coming as it did just after the deaths of President <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a> and General <a href="/wiki/Douglas_MacArthur" title="Douglas MacArthur">Douglas MacArthur</a>. Former <a href="/wiki/Chaplain_of_the_Senate" title="Chaplain of the Senate" class="mw-redirect">Chaplain of the Senate</a> <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Brown_Harris" title="Frederick Brown Harris">Frederick Brown Harris</a> officiated. All three had two things in common: the commanding general of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army_Military_District_of_Washington" title="United States Army Military District of Washington">Military District of Washington</a> during those funerals was Army Major General <a href="/wiki/Philip_C._Wehle" title="Philip C. Wehle">Philip C. Wehle</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Riderless_horse" title="Riderless horse">riderless horse</a> was <a href="/wiki/Black_Jack_(horse)" title="Black Jack (horse)">Black Jack</a>, who also served in that role during <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson" title="Lyndon B. Johnson">Lyndon B. Johnson</a>'s funeral.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Writing">Writing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Writing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Hoover began his magnum opus <i>Freedom Betrayed</i><sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146"><span>[</span>146<span>]</span></a></sup> in 1944 as part of a proposed autobiography. This turned into a significant work critiquing the foreign policy of the United States during the period from the 1930s to 1945. Essentially an attack on the statesmanship of <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a>, Hoover completed this work in his 90th year but it was not published until the historian <a href="/wiki/George_H._Nash" title="George H. Nash">George H. Nash</a> took on the task of editing it. Significant themes are his belief that the western democratic powers should have let Nazi Germany and Soviet Russia assail and weaken each other, and opposition to the British guarantee of Poland's independence.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147"><span>[</span>147<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Heritage_and_memorials">Heritage and memorials</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Heritage and memorials">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Herbert_Hoover_Presidential_Library_003.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Herbert_Hoover_Presidential_Library_003.jpg/220px-Herbert_Hoover_Presidential_Library_003.jpg" width="220" height="94" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Herbert_Hoover_Presidential_Library_003.jpg/330px-Herbert_Hoover_Presidential_Library_003.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Herbert_Hoover_Presidential_Library_003.jpg/440px-Herbert_Hoover_Presidential_Library_003.jpg 2x" data-file-width="633" data-file-height="270" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Herbert_Hoover_Presidential_Library_003.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hoover Presidential Library</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hoover_Plaque_Poznan.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Hoover_Plaque_Poznan.JPG/220px-Hoover_Plaque_Poznan.JPG" width="220" height="275" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Hoover_Plaque_Poznan.JPG/330px-Hoover_Plaque_Poznan.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Hoover_Plaque_Poznan.JPG/440px-Hoover_Plaque_Poznan.JPG 2x" data-file-width="842" data-file-height="1054" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hoover_Plaque_Poznan.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A plaque in Poznan honoring Herbert Hoover.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Hoover_Presidential_Library-Museum" title="Herbert Hoover Presidential Library-Museum" class="mw-redirect">Herbert Hoover Presidential Library and Museum</a> is located in <a href="/wiki/West_Branch,_Iowa" title="West Branch, Iowa">West Branch, Iowa</a> next to the <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Hoover_National_Historic_Site" title="Herbert Hoover National Historic Site">Herbert Hoover National Historic Site</a>. The library is one of thirteen presidential libraries run by the <a href="/wiki/National_Archives_and_Records_Administration" title="National Archives and Records Administration">National Archives and Records Administration</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Lou_Henry_and_Herbert_Hoover_House" title="Lou Henry and Herbert Hoover House" class="mw-redirect">Lou Henry and Herbert Hoover House</a>, built in 1919 in <a href="/wiki/Stanford,_California" title="Stanford, California">Stanford, California</a>, is now the official residence of the president of <a href="/wiki/Stanford_University" title="Stanford University">Stanford University</a>, and a <a href="/wiki/National_Historic_Landmark" title="National Historic Landmark">National Historic Landmark</a>. Hoover's rustic rural presidential retreat, Rapidan Camp (also known as Camp Hoover) in the <a href="/wiki/Shenandoah_National_Park" title="Shenandoah National Park">Shenandoah National Park</a>, Virginia, has been restored and opened to the public. The <a href="/wiki/Hoover_Dam" title="Hoover Dam">Hoover Dam</a> is named in his honor, as are numerous <a href="/wiki/Elementary_school" title="Elementary school">elementary</a>, <a href="/wiki/Middle_school" title="Middle school">middle</a>, and <a href="/wiki/High_school" title="High school">high schools</a> across the United States.</p>
<p>On December 10, 2008, Hoover's great-granddaughter <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Hoover" title="Margaret Hoover">Margaret Hoover</a> and <a href="/wiki/Senate_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Senate of Puerto Rico">Senate of Puerto Rico</a> President <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_McClintock" title="Kenneth McClintock">Kenneth McClintock</a> unveiled a life-sized bronze statue of Hoover at Puerto Rico's Territorial Capitol. The statue is one of seven honoring Presidents who have visited the United States territory during their term of office.</p>
<p>One line in the <i><a href="/wiki/All_in_the_Family" title="All in the Family">All in the Family</a></i> theme song—an ironic exercise in pre–New Deal nostalgia—says "Mister, we could use a man like Herbert Hoover again".</p>
<p>The Belgian city of <a href="/wiki/Leuven" title="Leuven">Leuven</a> named a square in the city center after Hoover, honoring him for his work as chairman of the "Commission for Relief in Belgium" during World War I. The square is near the Central Library of the Catholic University of Leuven, where a bust of the president can be seen.</p>
<p>The Polish capital of <a href="/wiki/Warsaw" title="Warsaw">Warsaw</a> also has a square named after Hoover alongside the Royal Route leading to the Old Town.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148"><span>[</span>148<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/George_Burroughs_Torrey" title="George Burroughs Torrey">George Burroughs Torrey</a> painted a portrait of him.</p>
<p>The historic townsite of Gwalia, Western Australia contains the Sons of Gwalia Museum and the Hoover House Bed and Breakfast, the renovated and restored Mining Engineers residence that was the original residence of Herbert Hoover and where he stayed in subsequent visits to the mine during the first decade of the twentieth century.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149"><span>[</span>149<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">

His former house in Gwalia is now a historical tourist attraction, and as of 2004, a bed and breakfast inn.
